## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: materials science – thermo-mechanical constitutive modeling
**Analysis**: The task involves stress (sigma) as a function of strain (epsilon) and temperature (T), which is the core of constitutive laws for the mechanical behavior of materials, especially plasticity and thermoelasticity. The relationship is typically governed by a hardening law combined with thermal softening, often exhibiting power-law, exponential, or linear coupling of strain and temperature.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Hooke's law (linear thermoelastic): sigma = c0 * epsilon + c1 * T (linear elasticity with temperature offset)
- Thermoelastic with thermal strain: sigma = c0 * (epsilon - c1 * T) (stress proportional to mechanical strain, subtracting thermal expansion)
- Linear hardening: sigma = c0 + c1 * epsilon (yield stress plus linear hardening)
- Power-law hardening (Ludwik): sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 (parabolic/exponential hardening, no temperature effect)
- Power-law hardening with thermal softening: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 * exp(-c2 * T) (combined strain hardening and Arrhenius-type thermal softening)
- Voce saturation hardening: sigma = c0 - (c0 - c1) * exp(-c2 * epsilon) (exponential saturation of flow stress with strain)
- Voce hardening with thermal softening: sigma = (c0 - c1 * exp(-c2 * epsilon)) * exp(-c3 * T) (saturation hardening scaled by thermal activation)
- Power-law with temperature-dependent exponent: sigma = c0 * epsilon**(c1 + c2 * T) (temperature modifies hardening rate)
- Johnson-Cook type (without strain rate): sigma = (c0 + c1 * epsilon**c2) * (1 - c3 * (T - c4)**c5) (yield plus hardening multiplied by thermal softening factor)
- Temperature-dependent modulus: sigma = (c0 - c1 * T) * epsilon (linear decrease in stiffness with temperature)
- Thermal softening of yield stress: sigma = c0 * exp(-c1 * T) + c2 * epsilon**c3 (yield stress decays exponentially with T, plus hardening)
- Double exponential hardening: sigma = c0 + c1 * (1 - exp(-c2 * epsilon)) + c3 * (1 - exp(-c4 * epsilon)) (multiple hardening stages)
- Generalized exponential saturation: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 * exp(-c2 * epsilon) (strain hardening followed by flow softening/damage)
- Multiplicative coupling: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 * (1 - c2 * T) (power-law hardening with linear thermal softening)
- Hyperbolic sine creep relation (inverse): sigma = c0 * asinh(c1 * epsilon * exp(c2 / T)) (considers temperature-activated viscous flow, but uses strain as proxy for strain rate; asinh ≈ log(x+sqrt(x^2+1)))

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Thermal softening: stress decreases with increasing temperature, often captured by exp(-c*T) or (1 - c*(T - T_ref)) factors multiplying the strain hardening term.
- Strain hardening saturation: if flow stress approaches a steady value, an exponential saturation form sigma = c0 - c1*exp(-c2*epsilon) is typical.
- Linear thermoelasticity: when strain is small, stress is linear in both epsilon and T, possibly offset by thermal expansion: sigma = c0*(epsilon - c1*T).
- Temperature-modulus effect: elastic modulus often decreases linearly with temperature, leading to sigma = (c0 - c1*T)*epsilon.
- Coupling via exponent: temperature may change the hardening exponent, giving epsilon**(c0 + c1*T), which captures the effect that materials soften more rapidly at high T.
- Yield stress dominance: in some cases, the strain contribution is negligible and sigma primarily depends on T via an exponential decay (thermal activation of yield).
- Recovery/recrystallization: at high temperatures, flow stress may first increase then decrease with strain, modeled by epsilon**c1 * exp(-c2*epsilon).
- Power-law dominance: many constitutive laws in plasticity assume a power-law relation between true stress and true strain, optionally scaled by thermal factors.
- Additive decomposition: total stress may be split into a thermal part and a mechanical part: sigma = f1(T) + f2(epsilon) or sigma = f1(epsilon)*f2(T).
- Boundary behavior: at zero strain stress may be non-zero (yield), so consider a constant term; at zero temperature thermal softening factor should approach 1.

Candidates with NO T-dependence at all (only epsilon): 36, 37, 39, 40, 41, 44, 45, 46, 47, 49, 50, 51, 53, 55, 64, 69, 83, 88
These all CANNOT match GT since GT depends on T.
